   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d22875568" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>1.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NMFPP), with its low cost and high energy density, is essential for accelerating the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries. However, its practical application is limited by serious voltage hysteresis and detrimental Jahn-Teller distortions. Herein, a high operating voltage and superior stable Nb-doped NMFPP with fewer intrinsic anti-site defects are elaborately designed by the reconstruction of the crystal lattice and electronic distribution. By introducing higher charge density Nb─O bonds, the lengths of Mn-O bonds are shortened, enhancing lattice stability. As a result, the lattice volume contracted during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion is decreased with niobium-modified Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2.94</sub>Nb<sub>0.06</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, mitigating lattice distortion from the Jahn-Teller effect and increasing the capacity retention after 1000 cycles from 57.5% to 82.3%. More importantly, the delayed effect of Mn<sup>2+</sup> involvement in redox reactions is significantly reduced, raising the average operating voltage from 3.32 to 3.64&nbsp;V and increasing the overall energy density by 13%. This study opens new avenues to develop advanced sodium-ion battery cathode materials with high energy density and long calendar life for energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410992-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The reliable and stable large-scale energy storage technology is the foundation for the replacement of traditional fossil fuels with new energy sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0001_R_d22875506e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0002_R_d22875506e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Convers. Manage.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increased demand for energy storage devices, sodium-ion batteries have become highly attractive candidates due to the abundance of sodium resources. To advance the practical application and industrialization of sodium-ion batteries, developing low-cost cathode materials with long cycle life and high safety has become the primary challenge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0003_R_d22875506e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0004_R_d22875506e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion battery polyanion cathode materials have attracted significant attention due to their excellent structural stability, electrochemical performance, safety, and abundant resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0005_R_d22875506e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are two typical polyanion cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Olivine-type NaFePO<sub>4</sub> has a structure similar to LiFePO<sub>4</sub> and a comparable theoretical capacity. However, it is difficult to synthesize directly through conventional solid-state methods and tends to convert to the electrochemically inactive maricite structure. Therefore, it is commonly obtained from LiFePO<sub>4</sub> through a complex ion-exchange process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0006_R_d22875506e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, which is easy to obtain, maintains well-defined channel structures (triclinic framework under the <i>P</i>1 space group). It benefits from the strong inductive effect of the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub><sup>4−</sup> group, resulting in a higher working voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0007_R_d22875506e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large polyanion group also limits its theoretical capacity. Mixed phosphate compounds combine the advantages of phosphate and pyrophosphate, not only providing suitable operating voltage and capacity but also experiencing minimal volume changes during cycling, thus garnering widespread attention. In recent years, the iron-based mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) has become a hot topic of research due to its excellent 3D structural stability, environmental friendliness, appropriate theoretical capacity (129&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and outstanding cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0008_R_d22875506e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0010_R_d22875506e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formation of Na defects and charge rearrangement was induced by incorporating inert Al<sup>3+</sup> into the NFPP lattice, and the Na<sub>3.9</sub>Fe<sub>2.9</sub>Al<sub>0.1</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode was meticulously designed by our research group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0011_R_d22875506e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Sci. Bull.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The material showed outstanding cycling stability (85.1% capacity retention over 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 50&nbsp;C), remarkable rate capability (41.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200&nbsp;C), and all-weather resistance (−40 and 60&nbsp;°C).</p>
         
         <p>Despite these advances, the redox potential of Fe in NFPP is only 3.0&nbsp;V, which seriously affects the energy density of the cathode. To improve the working voltage, manganese-based materials with high redox potentials are considered to be the best alternatives to Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0012_R_d22875506e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The average voltage plateau of pure manganese-based mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NMPP) can reach 3.8&nbsp;V, and the theoretical capacity is comparable to iron-based materials, significantly enhancing overall energy density. However, the replacement of Fe with Mn means the possible sacrifice of long-term stability. Distortion in the MnO6 octahedra is caused by the Jahn-Teller effect originating from Mn<sup>3+</sup>, leading to lattice strain and reduced structural stability, which in turn worsens cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0013_R_d22875506e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To ensure higher working potential as well as a longer lifespan, a manganese-iron-based mixed phosphate compound Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>3−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (0&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;x&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;3) (NMFPP) cathode is proposed. Compared with NFPP, NMFPP exhibits a higher working potential and increased energy density. Furthermore, in comparison with NMPP, the stability of NMFPP is greatly improved. Na<sub>4</sub>MnFe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> has been found to achieve an average working potential of 3.4&nbsp;V, with a capacity retention rate of 83% after 3000 cycles at 1&nbsp;C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0014_R_d22875506e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, due to the complex structure, unique ionic bonding, and low conductivity of NMFPP, the migration rate of manganese ions is limited, causing voltage hysteresis. This situation is commonly observed in manganese-based NASICON-type materials with 3D Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels, where large voltage hysteresis appears in the charge/discharge curves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0015_R_d22875506e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0016_R_d22875506e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite efforts to design sophisticated conductive networks to reduce voltage hysteresis, this phenomenon persists.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0017_R_d22875506e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Today Energy.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0018_R_d22875506e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, research on NMFPP cathodes has been limited up to now, particularly in addressing voltage hysteresis and capacity decay.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, it has been reported that high valence ion doping in NASICON-type cathodes could trigger the increase of intrinsic anti-site defects (IASD) formation energy. High-valent Mo<sup>6+</sup> was doped into Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to increase the formation energy of IASD, thereby reducing defect concentration and addressing the issue of voltage hysteresis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0019_R_d22875506e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Energy.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by this, Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping in NMFPP was proposed to address the voltage hysteresis issue. DFT calculations indicate that the charge density of Nb─O bonds is significantly higher than that of TM─O bonds, and lattice stability is enhanced. Additionally, the density of states calculations reveals that doping Nb at Fe sites significantly reduces the band gap, transforming the electronic structure into a half-metallic state in the majority spin channel, thereby enhancing electronic conductivity. Electrochemical tests show that compared to NMFPP, the average working voltage of NMFPPNb-2 is increased from 3.32 to 3.64&nbsp;V, and the cycling retention rate over 1000 cycles is improved from 57.5% to 82.3%. The issues of voltage hysteresis and reduced cycling stability caused by the Jahn-Teller effect are significantly mitigated. In situ XRD and synchrotron XANES tests reveal that the lattice volume change of the NMFPPNb-2 cathode is reduced from 5.06% to 2.96% due to Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping, reducing lattice distortion caused by the Jahn-Teller effect and alleviating the accumulation of local mechanical stress. Compared to NMFPP, structural stability is significantly enhanced. This study provides guidance for the design of future high-energy density, long-life NASICON phosphate cathodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410992-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>It is commonly believed that the high bond energy of Nb─O helps resist structural distortions and decomposition during the charge and discharge process, thereby extending the lifespan of the material. Additionally, Nb<sup>5+</sup> has a high charge density, enabling effective charge transfer with the surrounded oxygen atoms, which helps balance the internal charge distribution of the material, improving its electrical and ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0020_R_d22875506e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0021_R_d22875506e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To reveal how Nb<sup>5+</sup> reduces defect concentration and then mitigates voltage hysteresis, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were utilized. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0001">1a</a>, three cation substitution sites for Nb were explored, revealing a preference for the Fe site (−2.27&nbsp;eV) as the optimal position. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0001">1b</a> presents the crystal structure models of NMFPP and Nb-NMFPP before and after Nb substitution, respectively. Subsequently, electronic structure calculations were performed on the established models to evaluate the local electronic distribution within the structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0001">1c,d</a>). In NMFPP-Nb, more pronounced electron localization is exhibited by O coordination compared with NMFPP. The charge density of the Nb─O bond is significantly increased compared with the Fe─O bond in the original position, which enhances overall lattice stability due to the tighter Nb─O bond. Simultaneously, the Mn─O bond length is reduced from 1.567 to 1.564 Å, beneficially mitigating lattice distortion issues caused by the Jahn-Teller effect.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a5f1ee7-4bc8-4f9d-8c3d-934965866b55/adfm202410992-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a5f1ee7-4bc8-4f9d-8c3d-934965866b55/adfm202410992-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d96f228e-c98c-44f6-91a4-373a81ff7e32/adfm202410992-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a5f1ee7-4bc8-4f9d-8c3d-934965866b55/adfm202410992-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Calculations based on DFT on atomic and electronic structures of NMFPP and Nb-NMFPP. a) Formation energies of different sites of Nb in NMFPP lattice. b) Schematic crystal structures of NMFPP and optimized Nb-NMFPP. c) Partial charge density distribution on corresponding planes of NMFPP and Nb-NMFPP, respectively. d) The charge density difference of the Nb-NMFPP/ NMFPP framework; the yellow and cyan bubbles represent charge accumulation and depletion, respectively. e) The TDOS and PDOS. f) The comparison of activation energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in NMFPP and Nb-NMFPP, respectively. The inset is the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathway (yellow balls) in Nb-NMFPP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The total density of states (TDOS) and partial density of states (PDOS) indicate that the band gap between the valence band maximum (VBM) and the conduction band minimum (CBM) is 3.0&nbsp;eV before doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0001">1e</a>). Upon Nb doping at the Fe site, the formation of donor levels composed of electrons from the Nb cation below the Fermi level is observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0022_R_d22875506e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Int. J. Hydrogen Energy.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the electronic structure is transitioned to a half-metallic state in the majority spin channel, thereby enhancing the electronic conduction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0023_R_d22875506e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Mater. Res. Express.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the impact of Nb substitution on rate performance and cycling stability is estimated by calculating the energy barriers (<i>E</i><sub>α</sub>) for Na<sup>+</sup> migration along the basal axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0001">1f</a>). In NMFPP-Nb, the path from Na0 to Na6 is traversed by Na<sup>+</sup>, as depicted in the inset (the migration path for NMFPP in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with the maximum <i>E</i><sub>α</sub> of 0.78&nbsp;eV to overcome for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in NMFPP, the smaller <i>E</i><sub>α</sub> of 0.61&nbsp;eV is encountered by NMFPP-Nb, confirming the enhancement in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Additionally, the diffusion coefficients and conductivity of NMFPP and NMFPP-Nb were calculated based on <i>E</i><sub>α</sub> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The results reveal a remarkable enhancement in both ion diffusion coefficient and conductivity for NMFPP-Nb samples compared with pristine NMFPP.</p>
         
         <p>Supported by the theoretical calculations, it was expected that Nb<sup>5+</sup> modified NMFPP would exhibit excellent performance. A series of samples with different Nb doping levels (NMFPP, NMFPPNb-1 (1% Nd doping), NMFPPNb-2 (2% Nd doping), and NMFPPNb-3 (3% Nd doping)) were prepared utilizing the liquid-phase spray drying synthesis process. The significant differences in electrochemical behaviors are shown by NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0002">2a</a>, two distinct voltage plateaus at 3 and 4&nbsp;V are observed in the constant current charge–discharge curves of both NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2, corresponding to the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions, respectively. Although the positions of the voltage plateaus for NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 are consistent, large changes are observed in the A–B region of the figure. For NMFPP, there is serious voltage hysteresis in the 3–3.8&nbsp;V range, indicating fewer Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox reactions occur for the same Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. Similarly, region B exhibits the same trend. In contrast, with only 2% Nb doping, the average working voltage of NMFPPNb-2 was increased from 3.32 to 3.64&nbsp;V within the 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V range, eliminating voltage hysteresis and capacity loss. This phenomenon is widely noted in manganese-based NASICON-type cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0015_R_d22875506e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0024_R_d22875506e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the finding above, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0002">2b</a> describes the charge and discharge process of NMFPP, using color-coded squares to show how intrinsic anti-site defects (IASD) could affect Mn ions and cause voltage hysteresis. Initially, the Mn ions affected by IASD in NMFPP are marked by red squares. As Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted, the unaffected Mn ions have risen from +2 to +3 valence, but the affected Mn ions remain at +2 due to obstructed Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Thus, during further charging, the affected Mn ions coexist as +2 and +3 valence at the 4.0&nbsp;V plateau. The discharge process shows the same pattern, with the reaction being Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> → Delayed Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b851eaa-b2d1-464e-86bd-f813f627145f/adfm202410992-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b851eaa-b2d1-464e-86bd-f813f627145f/adfm202410992-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5eb1b4db-9c96-4054-ad49-85e8249c436f/adfm202410992-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b851eaa-b2d1-464e-86bd-f813f627145f/adfm202410992-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Voltage hysteresis of NMFPP. The second constant current cycle voltage curves at 0.1&nbsp;C for NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 in the 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V range. Insets A and B are magnified views of these regions, clearly showing the voltage hysteresis. b) Schematic of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion behavior. Blue and light blue squares indicate regions where manganese elements easily diffuse, while red and orange squares with red boxes indicate regions where manganese diffusion is hindered by intrinsic anti-site defects.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the factors causing this electrochemical difference, the structure and morphology of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 samples were characterized. Scanning electron microscope (SEM) results (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information) indicate that the morphology of the samples was not changed significantly before and after modification, both exhibiting a spherical structure of 0.5–2&nbsp;µm. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information) also demonstrates a uniform distribution of corresponding elements in both samples, with no introduction of other impurities. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information) reveals that the main material was uniformly coated with a carbon layer ≈3&nbsp;nm thick. The NMFPPNb-2 sample shows lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.2826&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (022) plane of NMFPP, while the NMFPP sample exhibited a lattice spacing of 0.2560&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (240) plane. The clear lattice fringes in both samples indicate good crystallinity and uniform crystal structure. Subsequently, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) was conducted on the four samples (NMFPP, NMFPPNb-1, NMFPPNb-2, and NMFPPNb-3) in an air atmosphere, showing mass losses of 5.73, 5.82, 5.84, and 5.94 wt.%, respectively. Further, Raman spectroscopy was used to characterize the carbon defects and degree of graphitization in NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2. The D and G bands observed at ≈1350 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), showed <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios of 1.472 for NMFPP and 1.438 for NMFPPNb-2. These results collectively show that the carbon content is similar across the four samples, and the degree of graphitization is not much different before and after modification, ruling out the smaller voltage hysteresis due to differences in carbon conductive network content and inherent structure. Subsequently, four groups of samples with different Nb doping levels were characterized using XRD (<i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i> space group, PDF#04-023-6754). The results showed no impurity peaks, indicating that the structure of NMFPP is not altered by low-dose Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3d</a>) In the NMFPP-Nb spectrum, the peak is shifted slightly toward higher diffraction angles compared to NMFPP, indicating lattice distortion, which could be attributed to the substitution of smaller Nb<sup>5+</sup> (0.7 Å) for Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) in the lattice sites. Moreover, the higher the substitution content, the more pronounced the shift in diffraction peaks becomes. In this case, as Nb<sup>5+</sup> exhibits a higher valence state, vacancies that facilitate fast ion/electron transport are created for charge balance. To delve deeper into the effects of Nb doping on the crystal structure, Rietveld refinement was performed on NMFPPNb-2 samples, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3e</a>. (Other samples were shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) The refinement results demonstrate a high agreement between experimental and calculated data, with low Rp (≈2.28%) and Rwp (≈2.91%) factors. The lattice parameters and atomic site information for NMFPPNb-2 and NMFPP are presented in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. A slight decrease in the a, b, and c lattice parameters and unit cell volume is generated by Nb doping, indicating that Nb<sup>5+</sup> was successfully doped into the structure of NMFPP. To further illustrate its structural characteristics, FT-IR testing was performed, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). It noted that the peak positions of the infrared curves of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 are consistent, confirming from the side that Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping does not change the crystal structure of NMFPP. Symmetric and asymmetric vibrations of P─O─P in the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups are located at 700–940 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0025_R_d22875506e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Mater. Today Nano.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 940–1300 cm<sup>−1</sup> spectral region consists mainly of symmetric and asymmetric stretching vibrations of P─O; the multiple bands in the 500–700 cm<sup>−1</sup> low-frequency range are from the bending vibration of O─P─O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0026_R_d22875506e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absorption ≈545 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from the M─O vibrations in isolated MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. It is evident that the peak intensity for the NMFPPNb-2 sample is higher due to the introduction of strong Nb─O bonds, agreeing well with theoretical calculations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6f20d8b-450e-4004-9213-e1fc4119b768/adfm202410992-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6f20d8b-450e-4004-9213-e1fc4119b768/adfm202410992-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/965d020c-5c69-42e7-9ead-9c9bb20d1260/adfm202410992-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6f20d8b-450e-4004-9213-e1fc4119b768/adfm202410992-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM image and b) HRTEM images of NMFPPNb-2. c) Typical SEM image with corresponding EDS elemental mappings. d) Powder XRD patterns of the samples. e) Rietveld refinements of NMFPPNb-2. XPS survey spectra f) full spectra, g) Nb 3d, h,i) Fe 2p, j,k) Mn 2p for NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further explore the valence states of Fe, Mn, and Nb elements in NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2, XPS measurement was carried out at room temperature. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3f</a>, both materials have binding energy peaks for Na 1s, Fe 2p, Mn 2p, P 2p, P 2s, O 1s, and C 1s, but the NMFPPNb-2 sample has an additional Nb 3d binding energy peak at about 208&nbsp;eV. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3h,i</a> displays the Fe 2p spectrum of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2. Two obvious peaks at 711.53 and 724.87&nbsp;eV are designated as Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0027_R_d22875506e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3j,k</a>, distinct peaks at 640.79 and 653.15&nbsp;eV, alongside a weak satellite peak at 645.88&nbsp;eV, are observed within the 637–657&nbsp;eV range, demonstrating the Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> transitions for Mn<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0028_R_d22875506e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Ceram. Int.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results of the curve-fitting spectra indicate that the relative amounts of Mn<sup>2+</sup> in NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 are 31.57% and 59.64%, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0029_R_d22875506e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Alloys Compd.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The activation of more redox-active Mn<sup>2+</sup> in NMFPP is facilitated by the introduction of Nb<sup>5+</sup>, which is beneficial in mitigating voltage hysteresis. The high-resolution Nb 3d spectra are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0003">3g</a>. The two split peaks at 206.83 and 209.54&nbsp;eV correspond to Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Nb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Nb<sup>5+</sup>, respectively, indicating that Nb in NMFPPNb-2 is +5 valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0030_R_d22875506e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electron concentration in the conduction band is increased and the band gap energy is reduced from 1.31 to 1.11&nbsp;eV by Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) and confirmed by Ultraviolet-visible diffuse reflectance spectroscopy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0031_R_d22875506e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0032_R_d22875506e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Nano">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings indicate that Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping alters charge distribution, enhancing electronic and ionic conductivity in NMFPPNb-2 compared to NMFPP.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage performance was evaluated in sodium half-cells within a voltage range of 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFMPP exhibit nearly overlapped initial five cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4a</a>), indicating high electrochemical reversibility. Additionally, two distinct sets of oxidation/reduction peaks are observed: 3.04/2.94&nbsp;V for the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox couple and 4.03/3.95&nbsp;V for the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> redox couple. Additionally, one minor oxidation peak emerged at approximately ≈2.85&nbsp;V, commencing from the second cycle of cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests. This phenomenon is due to the coordination number of sodium sites not being fixed, ranging from 5 to 7. Consequently, slight changes in the crystal field significantly affect the sodium intercalation potential of different sodium atoms, which is ultimately reflected as additional redox peaks in the CV curves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0033_R_d22875506e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 at different rates are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4b-c</a>. Compared with NFMPP, NMFPPNb-2 exhibits significantly improved rate performance. At rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;C, the discharge capacities of NMFPPNb-2 reached 101.1, 97.4, 93.1, 90.2, 85.3, 78.8, and 71.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, after high-rate testing, the corresponding cell of NMFPPNb-2 could recover back to a discharge capacity of 98.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when returning to 0.1&nbsp;C. At a low rate of 0.1&nbsp;C, the discharge-specific capacity of NFMPP reached 98.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, but it provided only 82.3 and 58.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 1 and 10&nbsp;C, respectively. This notable rate capability difference is believed to primarily stem from the reduction in bandgap and enhancement in Na<sup>+</sup> migration caused by Nb substitution. The capacities of NMFPPNb-1 and NMFPPNb-3 at different rates are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4d</a>, indicating that their rate performance is inferior to that of NMFPPNb-2, but both superior to NMFPP, particularly at high rates. Further investigation of the cyclic stability of the four sets of materials under different current densities was conducted, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4e</a>. As a result, the NMFPP electrode only retained 78.1% of its original capacity, giving a capacity of 65.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. However, NMFPPNb-2 maintained a reversible capacity of 81.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, coupled with a higher capacity retention of 90.3% under the same cycling condition. Moreover, all the samples were further investigated for long-term cyclability at a higher rate of 5&nbsp;C. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4f</a>, an unstable cycle was characterized by visible capacity deterioration for NMFPP, merely 57.5% of the initial capacity was retained with a prolonged cycle life of up to 1000 cycles. Moreover, severe capacity and voltage decay were clearly observed for the NMFPP electrode during cycling, indicating its pronounced structural/interface degradation. In contrast, the capacity retention of Nb-doped cathodes has been effectively improved, which of NMFPPNb-1, NMFPPNb-2, and NMFPPNb-3 are 75.9%, 82.3%, and 77.1% after 1000 cycles, respectively. Accordingly, a stable Coulombic Efficiency close to 100% was demonstrated by NMFPPNb-2, whereas the CE for NMFPP is highly variable, further suggesting the effectiveness and superiority of Nb-modification. Noteworthy, substantial mitigation of capacity/voltage decay was achieved for the Nb-doped NMFPPNb-2 cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4h,i</a>). The stability is further confirmed by its well-maintained crystal structure. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the NMFPPNb-2 and NMFPP cathode sheets were collected from half-cells after 1000 cycles and characterized via XRD. The results indicate that the peak heights of the cycled NMFPPNb-2 cathode match those in the XRD spectra, confirming the ability of NMFPPNb-2 to maintain a stable crystal structure during prolonged Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. In contrast, the NMFPP cathode exhibits significant structural degradation, especially in the (022) and (602) characteristic peaks, which will directly affect the material's electrochemical performance. The enhancement in cycling performance of Nb-doped samples is attributed to defects induced by Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping within the lattice, modifying conductivity and ion diffusion properties, while also mitigating the Jahn-Teller effect, consequently improving the efficacy of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0034_R_d22875506e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0036_R_d22875506e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, Nb<sup>5+</sup>, being intermediate in size between Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>, aids in the transition of manganese from divalent to trivalent, which alleviates structural collapse caused by manganese valence state conversion, rendering the material more stable. Consequently, manganese dissolution is effectively inhibited.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0037_R_d22875506e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Energy Chem.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4g</a> illustrates the discharge energy density of the four samples. With the improvement in voltage hysteresis and the enhancement of discharge capacity, the energy density of NMFPPNb-2 reaches 320&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C, significantly surpassing the energy density of NMFPP at 283&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, hereby achieving a 13% increase. These findings offer new insights for the development of low-cost, high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ec8a10ed-c6e3-487f-8be3-007896336a0e/adfm202410992-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ec8a10ed-c6e3-487f-8be3-007896336a0e/adfm202410992-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/94969a37-ec7b-43a3-90a4-1bc84ba9f79a/adfm202410992-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ec8a10ed-c6e3-487f-8be3-007896336a0e/adfm202410992-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV plots at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of NMFPPNb-2 within 1.7–4.3&nbsp;V. NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2. b,c) GCD curves at different rates. d) Rate capability and e,f) cycling performance of NMFPPNb-x (x = 0, 1, 2, and 3) samples. g) Specific energy at 1&nbsp;C. The discharging profiles with increased cycles at 5 C between 1.7 − 4.3&nbsp;V for h) NMFPP, and i) NMFPPNb-2 electrodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further explore the enhanced electrochemical performance, electron transfer, and sodium ion diffusion kinetics analyses were conducted on various cathodes. NMFPPNb-2 displayed smaller polarization peaks in CV curves over NMFPP at different scan rates, suggesting the improved reversibility resulted from Nb doping (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). Furthermore, the integrated area under the CV curves for NMFPPNb-2 is larger, consistent well with its demonstrated superior capacity performance. Additionally, the Na<sup>+</sup> migration rates of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 are calculated from the CV curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, with calculation details provided in Supporting Information Note). The results depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5e</a> demonstrate a remarkable enhancement in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics for NMFPPNb-2 after modification. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5f</a>. In comparison to NMFPP, the diameter of the semicircles corresponding to the three cathodes after Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping are significantly reduced, indicating lower charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>). Moreover, the slope of the low-frequency region represents the diffusion impedance of ions toward the electrode surface in the electrolyte. NMFPPNb-2 exhibited the steepest slope in the low-frequency region, signifying faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport rates, in line with its rate performance. Additionally, the ion insertion/extraction kinetics during charge and discharge were assessed through GITT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5g</a>). The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during charge and discharge processes are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5h,i</a> (calculation details provided in Supporting Information Note), wherein NMFPPNb-2 exhibits significantly increased Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at various states of charge (SOCs) compared to NMFPP. This further emphasizes the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> transport performance and material conductivity after Nb modification. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0005">5j,k</a> respectively illustrate single titration selections of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 at ≈3.1&nbsp;V. After Nb doping, it is evident that the values of ΔEτ and ΔEs decrease, further substantiating the mitigation of electrochemical polarization in the NMFPPNb-2 cathode and the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion capability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aa50a0f2-5c75-4d22-8a41-3fdb61c28098/adfm202410992-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/aa50a0f2-5c75-4d22-8a41-3fdb61c28098/adfm202410992-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/999f15a1-5c20-461e-9db0-4bbdd81d018e/adfm202410992-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aa50a0f2-5c75-4d22-8a41-3fdb61c28098/adfm202410992-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 a,b) CV plots at different scan rates, and c,d) linear curves between <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c2fde3e6-f4aa-4ce0-a130-c591315ccd18/adfm202410992-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202410992:adfm202410992-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202410992-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mfrac>
                           </msup>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{v}^{\frac{1}{2}}}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math> and <i>I<sub>p</sub></i>. e) The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics based on CV tests. f) EIS spectra. g) GITT plot. h,i) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics at different voltages based on GITT. j,k) The polarization curve of GITT at 3.1&nbsp;V.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Through in&nbsp;situ XRD measurements (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0006">6a</a>), the structural evolution of NMFPPNb-2 is observed during charging/discharging between 1.7 and 4.3&nbsp;V. (The corresponding stacked XRD patterns are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Throughout the process, continuous peak shifts in the XRD patterns are observed. During charging, characteristic peaks such as (410), (020), (022), (222), and (612) all exhibit a slight inclination toward higher 2θ angles, attributed to the slight contraction of the unit cell. Conversely, during discharging, these characteristic peaks return back to their initial 2θ angles. The entire process is highly reversible, indicative of a single-phase solid solution phase transition. It is noteworthy that the Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation behavior of pure iron-based Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) belongs to a non-ideal solid solution phase transition, exhibiting slight discontinuous peak shifts at the end of charging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0038_R_d22875506e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Fuels">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0039_R_d22875506e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, pure manganese-based Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) undergoes multiphase reactions during electrochemical reactions, with two intermediate phases (β and δ-phases).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0040_R_d22875506e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The observed difference may be attributed to the uniform distribution of Mn and Fe elements within the crystal, which promotes continuous interactions between the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> reactions. This results in a complete solid solution mechanism with equal Mn and Fe content. Similar phenomena have been reported in LMFP, where a more uniform distribution of Mn and Fe promotes stronger and more significant mutual electronic structural effects during redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0041_R_d22875506e1315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0006">6b</a> gives a comparison of the changes in the (022) and (222) characteristic peaks of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 during charge and discharge processes. Throughout the entire charge–discharge cycle, the (022) and (222) peaks of both samples are initially shifted toward higher diffraction angles and then returned back to their initial positions. Notably, the maximum shifts in NMFPPNb-2 for (022) and (222) are observed to be 0.699° and 0.721°, respectively, which are much smaller than those of NMFPP (0.887° and 1.000°, respectively), indicating that the volume change is alleviated by the introduction of Nb. Furthermore, the lattice volume changes during the charge–discharge processes of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2 electrodes were analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0006">6c</a>). During charging, they both show initial slight increases followed by rapid decreases. However, during discharging, NMFPPNb-2 exhibits a steady increase in lattice volume. This trend aligns with the variation of lattice parameters along the b-axis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The lattice volume change of the positive electrode of NMFPPNb-2 decreases from 5.06% to 2.96% after Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping, indicating a significant enhancement in structural stability compared to NMFPP.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62bdcff9-989c-47ec-b937-aed3ddb1eb30/adfm202410992-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/62bdcff9-989c-47ec-b937-aed3ddb1eb30/adfm202410992-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4347b266-a533-4b45-a2f1-f8e57a4602da/adfm202410992-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62bdcff9-989c-47ec-b937-aed3ddb1eb30/adfm202410992-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD analysis characterizations of NMFPPNb-2 during the charge/discharge process. b) Contour plots of in&nbsp;situ XRD patterns and c) cell volume changes upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction of NMFPP and NMFPPNb-2. d) The Fe and Mn k-edge XANES spectra of NMFPPNb-2 electrodes collected at different charge/discharge reactions in Na-ion cells. e) Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra of the Fe and Mn K-edge.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Through XANES and EXAFS analysis, the alterations in the local coordination environment and corresponding valence state of transition metals during charge/discharge processes were further investigated. The XANES spectra at Fe and Mn K-edges for NMFPPNb-2 are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0006">6d</a>. Notably, during charging from the initial state to 4.3&nbsp;V, a marked shift toward higher energy is shown in the Fe K-edge spectra, indicative of oxidation from Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. In contrast, minimal changes are displayed in the Mn K-edge spectra during charging, with only a slight rightward shift noted at 4.3&nbsp;V in the inset of the figure, indicating possibly delayed kinetics of the Mn<sup>2+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0042_R_d22875506e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Phys. Chem. C">42</a>-<a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0044_R_d22875506e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Electrochim. Acta.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharge to 1.7&nbsp;V, both Fe and Mn K-edges return back to their initial positions, highlighting the high reversibility of their redox processes. The Fourier-transformed (FT) EXAFS spectra are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0006">6e</a>. During the desodiation process, a noticeable reduction in atomic distances is observed at the Fe K-edge, corresponding to Fe oxidation; conversely, changes are less evident at the Mn K-edge, consistent with the explanation above. Overall, reversible changes in TMO<sub>6</sub> (TM = Fe and Mn) octahedra and a stable mixed phosphate framework contribute to the excellent cycling performance of NMFPPNb-2.</p>
         
         <p>To assess the practical potential of NMFPPNb-2, NMFPPNb-2//HC full cells were assembled (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0007">7a</a>). Due to the poor performances of hard carbon (HC) in lipid-based electrolytes (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC (1:1) +5%FEC), particularly at high current densities, ether-based electrolytes (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME) were employed as the evaluation system for the full cells (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Excellent rate performance based on the hard carbon anode was not only guaranteed by this electrolyte but also a similar average operating voltage to the half-cells was maintained.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0045_R_d22875506e1400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To mitigate the adverse impact of the low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC on the overall cell performance, pre-sodiation treatment was applied to the anode in the half-cell configuration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0046_R_d22875506e1405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the sodium-pre-sodiated HC anode was paired with the NMFPPNb-2 cathode to assemble the full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0007">7b</a>). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0007">7c</a>, the dQ/dV curves of the NMFPPNb-2//HC full cell reveal two pairs of oxidation/reduction peaks at 2.78/2.76&nbsp;V and 3.95/3.90&nbsp;V, respectively. Compared to the half cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0004">4a</a>), a slight decrease in the oxidation/reduction potential at Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> is observed in the NMFPPNb-2//HC full cell, which could be attributed to the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon. Additionally, the well-overlapped dQ/dV curves confirm excellent reversibility during the charge–discharge processes of the full cell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0007">7d,e</a> illustrates the rate performance of NMFPPNb-2//HC full cells, delivering discharge-specific capacities of 94.4, 91.2, 86.5, 81.7, 75.6, and 62.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5&nbsp;C, respectively, with remarkable rate capability. It is worth noting that the capacity of the full cell is based on the cathode weight. Furthermore, the long cycle performance of the NMFPPNb-2//HC full cell was conducted at a 1 C rate. Results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410992-fig-0007">7f,g</a>) reveal that 87.2% of the initial capacity is retained after 150 cycles with a cycling efficiency stable above 99.5%, suggesting excellent cycling durability of the full cells. Therefore, the outstanding electrochemical performance exhibited by NMFPPNb-2//HC full cells further validates the potential value of NMFPPNb-2 in practical applications of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410992-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4cf7dd6a-3bee-44f0-848d-36b7ed228848/adfm202410992-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4cf7dd6a-3bee-44f0-848d-36b7ed228848/adfm202410992-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b6c6c7b5-d970-40ca-8500-0f4bc6751501/adfm202410992-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4cf7dd6a-3bee-44f0-848d-36b7ed228848/adfm202410992-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410992-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410992" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Normalized GCD profiles of NMFPPNb-2 and HC. b) The schematic illustration of NMFPPNb-2||HC full cell. c) The corresponding dQ/dV curves at 0.2 C between 1.7 and 4.3&nbsp;V. d) Rate performance with e) corresponding GCD plots of the full cell. f) The cycling performance, and g) the corresponding charge/discharge curves at 1&nbsp;C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410992-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, to address the voltage hysteresis and Jahn-Teller effect issues in NMFPP, the electronic distribution and lattice have been rationally reconstructed by introducing Nb<sup>5+</sup> to reduce the intrinsic anti-site defects in manganese-iron-based phosphate cathodes. Density functional theory calculations reveal that the tighter Nb-O bonds enhance lattice stability, while the shortened Mn-O bonds alleviate lattice distortion caused by the Jahn-Teller effect. Additionally, density of states calculations and Kubelka−Munk transformed ultraviolet absorption spectra have confirmed that Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping alters the charge distribution, enhancing both electronic and ionic conductivity in NMFPPNb-2. Furthermore, it is noticed from the electrochemical performance test that the Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox reaction is promoted and voltage hysteresis is significantly reduced, raising the average working voltage from 3.32 to 3.64&nbsp;V. in&nbsp;situ XRD test results indicate that a single-phase solid solution reaction is exhibited by NMFPPNb-2 during sodium ion extraction, with highly reversible redox processes for Mn and Fe. Compared with NMFPP, lattice strain is effectively mitigated by NMFPPNb-2, with a volume change of only 2.96%, showing significantly improved structural stability. Consequently, excellent rate capability and strong cycling stability are exhibited by the NMFPPNb-2 cathode. This study provides a viable strategy for advancing NASICON-type phosphate cathodes and accelerating the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410992-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202410992-sec-0070-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> was synthesized via a spray drying method followed by subsequent high-temperature annealing. Commonly, 0.06&nbsp;mol Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Macklin, &gt;99.5%), 0.045&nbsp;mol Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin, &gt;99.9%), 0.045&nbsp;mol Mn(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin, &gt;99.0%), 0.054&nbsp;mol citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), 0.006&nbsp;mol ascorbic acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) and 0.12&nbsp;mol NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (Macklin, &gt;99.0%) were weighed according to the stoichiometric ratio and dissolved in 150&nbsp;mL deionized water. The slurry was stirred thoroughly using a magnetic stirrer until it transformed into a clear and transparent yellow-green solution. In this way, citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) and ascorbic acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) served as carbon sources and complexing agents. Subsequently, the dispersion solution was spray-dried with an inlet air temperature of 220&nbsp;°C, resulting in a brown powder precursor with uniform carbon-coating. The above precursor powder was pre-heated at 350&nbsp;°C for 1&nbsp;h, then calcined at 600&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;h with a heating rate of 3&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> under an Ar-H<sub>2</sub> (95/5 vol.%) atmosphere to obtain the final carbon decorated Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NMFPP). Thereafter, the Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2.97</sub>Nb<sub>0.03</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2.94</sub>Nb<sub>0.06</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>(Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2.91</sub>Nb<sub>0.09</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> samples (NMFPPNb-1, NMFPPNb-2 and NMFPPNb-3, respectively) were synthesized using the same process, except some of the C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>4</sub>NNbO<sub>9</sub>·xH<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin, &gt;99.99%) was proportionally replaced with Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O in the original raw materials.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202410992-sec-0080-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The composition was recorded by X-ray diffractometry (XRD, Rigaku Ultima IV, Japan). The phase composition was analyzed using Rietveld refinement applied by GSAS and EXPGUI software. The microstructure and morphology were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachi Regulus8100) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100). Surface elemental analysis was performed by X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS, ESCALAB 250Xi, Al-Kα). Raman spectra were measured using a NEXUS 670 FT-IR Raman spectrometer. Chemical bonding vibrations were collected by Fourier Transform Infrared Spectrometer (FTIR, Thermo Scientific Nicolet iS5, USA). Ultraviolet-visible diffuse reflectance spectra were collected using UV–vis diffuse reflectance spectroscopy (UV-vis/DRs, UV-3600). Carbon contents were evaluated via thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) instrument (STA 2500 Regulus). In situ, XRD measurements were conducted at 0.2&nbsp;C during the initial cycle using a specialized device with a Beryllium window. The X-ray absorption spectra of Fe and Mn K-edges were recorded at the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202410992-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical properties of the cathode materials were evaluated using CR2016 half-coin cells. The active substance, conductive agent (super-P), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) with a mass proportion of 80:10:10 were thoroughly mixed in N-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) to form the cathode slurry. The slurry was then coated on aluminum foil and dried under vacuum at 120&nbsp;°C for 10 h. The loading density of the cathode material was ≈1.8&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The coin-type half-cell was assembled with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (EC: DMC = 1:1 v/v) with 5 wt.% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), Na metal (diameter: 16&nbsp;mm), a cathode disc (diameter: 14&nbsp;mm), and glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman, diameter: 19&nbsp;mm) in an argon-filled glove box. The concentrations of H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> were maintained below 0.01&nbsp;ppm. The full cell followed the capacity matching principle, precisely aligning the N/P ratio between the cathode and anode to 1.2–1.3. Prior to full-cell fabrication, negative discs were pre-sodiated with sodium metal in a half-cell, undergoing pre-sodiation through two cycles at low current density (50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The full-cell system was evaluated using a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/diethylene glycol dimethyl ether solution. Constant-current charge/discharge curves were collected on a Neware battery testing system (Neware Co., Ltd, Shenzhen) over a voltage window from 1.7 to 4.3&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, 1&nbsp;C = 90&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were performed by charging/discharging at C/10 for 10 min with a rest period of 40 min. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were recorded using the Autolab electrochemical workstation (MULTI AUTOLAB M204) at various scan rates, while the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was measured using the CHI660E electrochemical workstation over a frequency range from 0.01&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz. All tests were conducted at 25&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202410992-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202410992-sec-0100-title"> Density Functional Theory Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>In this investigation, all computations were conducted using the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) with the projector augmented wave (PAW) method. The Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functional, in conjunction with the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) method, was employed for the exchange-correlation functional, augmented by the DFT-D3 correction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0047_R_d22875506e1694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Chem. Phys.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The plane-wave basis cut-off energy was established at 500&nbsp;eV. For computational procedures, Brillouin zone integration was implemented with 1&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;3 Gamma-centre k-point sampling. The generation of bulk models, incorporating partial occupation sites of Mn and Fe, was facilitated by the Alloy Theoretic Automated Toolkit (ATAT), particularly utilizing the special quasi-random structures (SQS) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410992-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410992-bib-0048_R_d22875506e1699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Calphad">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Partial occupancy of Mn and Fe Coulombic interaction (DFT+U) was applied to the transition elements, employing a U-J value of 4&nbsp;eV. Self-consistent calculations were subjected to a convergence energy threshold of 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV and equilibrium geometries were refined with maximum stress on each atom within 0.02&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. The determination of diffusion barriers for Na-ion migration within the structures was achieved through the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method, incorporated within the VASP transition state tools (VTST).</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1" id="adfm202410992-bibl-0001">
               <ul class="rlist separator">
                  
                  <li data-bib-id="adfm202410992-bib-0001"><span class="bullet">1</span><span class="author">A. A. Kebede</span>, <span class="author">T. Kalogiannis</span>, <span class="author">J.
